### **Related passage (related entity: Helsinki):** 
Helsinki has a population of about 515,000 people. It is one of the most affluent and modern cities in Northern Europe, with plenty of culture and interesting events. The capital of Finland also has its very own language, which is called Finnish. In addition to the Finnish language, Helsinki has English as official language and also German, Russian, French, Dutch and other foreign languages are spoken. The city also hosts many international organizations; for example the Nordic Council (formerly the Scandinavian Union) meets here every year. Also the World Bank office holds their meetings at the congress center of Helsinki, and there are several important organisations based here. The city centre offers lots of shopping opportunities and great restaurants where you can get some delicious dishes from all over the world. This city has many attractive places and sights that attract tourists and locals alike. For instance the main railway station is built on an island and the harbour area is one of the best preserved in the northern hemisphere. Other attractions include parks and museums, like the Ateneum art museum, the famous Kiasma contemporary art museum, the Museum of Contemporary Art, the national history museum of Finland, and others. Finland's biggest lake by area, Hämeenlinna, located just west of Helsinki, is popular among those who want to go on a boating trip or enjoy fishing. You'll find the beautiful lakeside village of Hanko, which offers nice beaches, cafés, shops and small guesthouses. There is also a new large open air theatre, designed by architect Eero Ruoho, in the village park. Helsinki is served by well developed public transport system including railways, buses and ferries. Ferries operate between Helsinki and Estonia. There is a direct train route between Helsinki and St Petersburg in Russia. The metro network covers much of the city but it is still incomplete so don't count on using this transport system often, especially on weekend evenings and holidays when crowds grow bigger than usual. For getting around the city more easily, bus services operate within the central part and the neighbouring municipalities, while taxis work within the city limits only. Car rentals are available at airports and train stations. The major roads are well-marked and they connect Helsinki with almost all parts of Finland. Helsinki also has several bicycle sharing schemes and these include bicycles that you can use for free or that have a charge depending on how long you cycle or ride. As Finland is in the north, Helsinki experiences four seasons and there are often heavy snowfalls. When snow falls the whole city gets covered in a layer of fluffy white stuff and makes for some quite spectacular pictures if taken during winter. On days when there is no sun, Helsinki is dark and cloudy. Summers are relatively short and pleasant and there is little rain and even less humidity because of the Gulf Stream. Spring is a wonderful time of year, when wild flowers bloom on hills and plains and there is a lot of outdoor music. Autumn is the best time of the year, when colours begin to change and the days become shorter and colder. The mild and sunny winters make travelling easier - although there may be some foggy weather in January. Winters are rather chilly and snowy. However, the cold does not stop people from enjoying themselves and going out and doing things. Helsinki is a very clean city, without much pollution. All kinds of cars, bikes and electric vehicles run freely throughout the city, though parking spaces are limited. Even bicycles are permitted to use certain roads at certain times of the day. A local government was created as early as 1788. Its first mayor was Carl Johan Adlercreutz who later became a professor at the University of Helsinki and after him came Lars Magnus Holmström who became one of the leading architects in Finland. Today the current Helsinki City Hall building was designed by the renowned architect Alvar Aalto. The building is shaped in an abstract style similar to the works he made before 1930 and resembles nothing else but a big glass bottle. He died in 1964 and now the building belongs to the City of Helsinki. His former studio "Kaleva" is kept open to visitors. It looks like a typical artist's workshop inside and serves as exhibition space and lecture room. One should notice the design of the wall panels where sketches were pasted when he worked on different projects. Helsinki had to withstand attacks on numerous occasions and today is considered to be one of the safest cities in Europe. This is mainly due to the good relations it maintains with nearby Estonia and Russia; both neighbours of Finland used to be enemies during the Cold War. After the war ended, diplomatic ties with Finland remained strong. The two countries share a common border with Sweden, Norway and Russia and have established friendly relations in the fields of defence, energy security and economic cooperation. Finland has been a member of NATO since 1949 and is a member of European Union. As a result of joining the EU, Helsinki joined Schengen Area in 2007 and introduced visa requirements for citizens of most western countries including France

### **Main passage (subject: Rantum):**
Rantum is located on a small island to the south of the coast. Its geographical situation gives it some advantages, among them being that it does not have to contend with coastal swells, and that it's sheltered by sea from much of the prevailing wind. It also has quite good anchorage. The island's position also creates disadvantages; it is very exposed to northerly winds, which create strong currents and waves. This makes it a poor haven for yachts and other small boats. The winds also create strong tides and it can be dangerous sailing at low tide. Finally, the lack of good anchorages makes it hard to find a safe spot to anchor close to shore. This all means that yacht owners seeking refuge and relaxation in Rantum should try to take advantage of the weather when conditions are favourable, or be prepared to move if the weather turns against them. History For the purposes of this study of ancient Greece, the period referred to here is the 6th century BC to 200 years after Christ, usually termed the "Classical" Period. From roughly 508 BC to 322 AD, Greece came under Persian rule; after 322, she again returned to independence. In 279 BC, Philip II of Macedon conquered the entire Greek area known as Macedonia; thus, most of the country comes within our dates; while Athens was independent until 404, the Peloponese were ruled by Sparta until 369, and Syracuse for nearly the same length of time. The Greeks who lived in mainland Greece during this period were known as Hellenistic Greeks, although they are often called by historians and antiquarians Greeks. After Alexander died and his empire broke up into smaller kingdoms, the Greek world was further divided, with various Greek tribes ruling the various areas. A number of new regions were also established, including Ptolemaic Egypt, Roman Syria, Pergamum, Pontus, Galatia, Asia Minor, Cappadocia, Armenia, Mesopotamia, Anatolia, Epirus, Thrace, Macedonia and Crete. In addition to these peoples, there were many others present in the Greco-Roman world, but they did not speak Greek. We may divide the Greek states into several major groups: (1) the Achaean League, based in central Greece, between Elis and Thessaly, including the cities of Sparta and Corinth, which included almost all of Laconia and Phocis, plus some parts of Achaea, Boeotia, Arcadia and East Attica; (2) the Antigonid Kingdom of Macedon (301–289), which extended across northern Greece from Macedonian borders to Thracian and Illyrian ones; and (3) the Seleucid Empire of Antiochus III, (281–246).  Antiquity There are three types of evidence available from ancient times used to determine the earliest form of Greek civilisation: • Ancient literature. This includes early myths and epics, literary texts and prose writings such as historical and philosophical documents, inscriptions, artefacts, statues, coins, and architecture. • Archaeological findings. These include tombs, gravesites and buildings, such as temples, sanctuaries, houses, streets, palaces and city walls. There are also tools used in farming, metalwork, pottery and manufacturing techniques. An archaeologist might also uncover evidence pertaining to the daily lives of ordinary people, such as diet, clothing and housing. • Contemporary accounts. ### **Related passage (related entity: Rantum Basin):** 
Rantum Basin is part of the Cretaceous Kjellerud Formation, which was deposited on a platform during a tropical period. Rantum is the main basin system of the Norwegian island of Svalbard (Figure 1). There are no glaciers in Svalbard and therefore Rantum is not glaciated as such but rather represents the main outlet for glacial waters. During early times, Rantum has been interpreted as a closed sea with a permanent freshwater influx from the surrounding Arctic shelf area. However, more recent evidence indicates that parts of the uppermost basins were opened at some point during the late Eocene.  During mid Cenozoic times, the whole Svalbard Platform and most of Barents Sea were covered by shallow seas that became isolated from each other during the uplift of the Scandinavian Mountains during Tertiary times. This was followed by the formation of several major bays within the ocean that would become filled by glacial meltwater, lakes, and rivers. Lake Agassiz formed in North America around 2 mya, while Lake Lillovik forms from the melting of the Fennoscandian ice sheet around 7 million years ago. Lake Agassiz drained into Hudson Bay or St. Lawrence River.  The first marine fossils date back to the Early Paleogene Period, about 56-66 million years ago. From about 65-25 Ma, this fauna is dominated by lobe-finned fish and ammonoids, followed by conodonts until the Mesozoic Era. The Late Jurassic to middle Miocene is characterised by warm tropical conditions (from 22-18Ma) in southern Europe; these were also present on Svalbard during early Cenozoic times, but then became gradually cooler from 15 Ma onwards. After 15 ma, however, the warm temperatures increased and remained in place until now, when the sea water temperature can be as high as 23oC in summer!  As can be seen from Figure 2, the upper levels of the lowermost unit consist mainly of a thick black shale, known as the Rantunshar Formation (Table 1), which contains fossilized remains of plants and animals. The second layer consists of organic sediment layers, mostly represented by well-preserved plant mats. These are known as the Galdhøpiggen sandstone. These sediments are the last remnants of the former ocean floor during the latest Cenozoic, when the ocean began to retreat from Svalbard because of rising global temperatures.  Tropical conditions persisted until about 2 Ma. The climate then started to cool, which led to a succession of changes in vegetation patterns across Europe and later on the Arctic Circle. The flora changed from evergreen shrubs to tree trees. The first tree species in the world to evolve from the C3 photosynthesis group was discovered in southern Norway, which today is known as Dendrophyton zschokkei. A few years later, the angiosperm pollen and leaf types, which form the basis of modern taxonomy, evolved.  After about 4 Ma, the Arctic Ocean began to shrink. With reduced water exchange between Atlantic and Pacific oceans, more oxygen entered the ocean. Because of lower oxygen content, fewer marine animal species could survive in surface waters, which caused many to move towards shallower depths. Consequently, the number of bottom dwelling species increased greatly during this time, and this continues even today.  Fossils indicate that there were extensive areas of temperate rainforest on the continental shelf around 30 Ma and even during the Miocene. The only record we have of the evolution of forest stands in northern latitudes is found in Northern Hemisphere boreal forests, which appeared around 15 Ma during a cooling stage, when the climate was much like it is today. As the climate began to change in the Pliocene Epoch, a period of dramatic biodiversity rise occurred. New animal groups and types of plants evolved and moved into new habitats. It was probably during this time that polar bears arose from their Asian relatives.  About 25 Ma, the climate became generally wetter than today. Glaciers expanded and snowfields grew larger. For example, glaciers reached the lowest land on Greenland's plateau at about 24 Ma. This is one of the reasons why so much ice still exists in Greenland today. In addition to this major environmental change, the sea level also rose by about 120 meters, reaching its maximum at 14.6 Ma.

### **Main passage (subject: Akrotiri and Dhekelia):**
Akrotiri and Dhekelia are two Greek island, situated on the eastern part of Cyprus. The distance between them is about 22 miles. Both islands are located among the Paphos group. However, Akrotiri is a more prominent place than Dhekelia. Besides, it’s name means “eagle mountain” for some reason. In any case, there’re just two small towns located on these places. They have not much to offer compared to other bigger cities around Cyprus like Nicosia, Limassol or Larnaca. People mainly go there because they want to see this beautiful coastline as well as enjoy its sea and beaches. As for me, I chose Akrotiri for my vacation spot while visiting these countries. It’s a little less popular than Dhekelia, but still, it doesn’t make it lesser interesting.  Dhekelia is also a rather unknown place. There’re only one small town on top of this small island (population 10 000). This island can be classified into a region that includes many others smaller islands like Tisamá, Skalofásni and Almyrida. Its climate is tropical, being similar to Malta’s climate. Moreover, it has great potentiality for tourism. Some resorts are already built here and people visit these during their holidays. It seems quite easy to reach, however, there’re no direct ferry lines. So, it requires a couple of hours by plane to travel to your destination. But let’s say you want to live here… It isn’t very common, at least not yet. However, it was an ancient city of Paphos area back in Byzantine times. It has been named after Cypriot martyr Dorotheus. He is known for his participation in the Council of Chalcedon that defined the Orthodox faith. That’s why he is said to be the patron saint of Cyprus. The church of Saint Dorotheus and a monastery were found here in 1952, which are now on display in the village museum. Even though there’s nothing new here nowadays, we’ll have to keep our eyes open for future plans of development. But still, it might happen!  Akrotiri on the other hand is a big tourist resort! I have visited this place years ago and it was really fun. You must know that Akrotiri is the capital of the Sovereign Base Areas or SBA in short (SBAs are areas belonging to the British military bases in Europe – mostly on the Mediterranean coast. You can read more about them here). Many tourists like to visit this place since it offers great things to do, such as scuba diving and sailing activities. The SBA itself is about 4 kilometres long and 1 kilometre wide. It takes up a lot of space and therefore there’s a limit on the number of buildings within. Most of them are houses or hotels to stay the night. And finally, Akrotiri consists of a main harbour and several harbours. Among those, there’s one with an airport. This airport is officially called Akrotiri Airport, however, it’s better to call it Rakiou Airport or Agios Dionysios Airport.  But all right, back to Akrotiri, there’re some nice restaurants and cafés where you can eat something decent (the prices are pretty cheap). We went down to the beach and spent some time swimming. Actually, it’s a very nice place. You can walk along the shoreline and find the best possible spots. When I came back home, my mother told me that she saw lots of people there without knowing who they were. Apparently, the same thing happened to us too. Well, if you don’t mind sharing a room with strangers or even having bad beds, maybe this place will suit you! If you prefer being alone then perhaps you shouldn’t bother going here.  And if you’re looking for the most pleasant days in Cyprus, don’t forget to check out this holiday destination when travelling between the two parts of the island. Akrotiri is in Paphos, which is in Kyrenia and is accessible via the Akoupa Road that connects the two places. Akoupa Road is the highest road of Cyprus and so you get a great view of this part of the island. Of course, Akrotiri was chosen for us, even though Dhekelia is closer to Paphos. After all, what else to choose?  I feel bad that the Akrotiri picture was taken at a time before the new Akrotiri airport was completed and the old one still functioned! ### **Related passage (related entity: British Cyprus):** 
British Cyprus, the Greek Cypriot side had one more issue, and that was on who would be in charge of their territory. The plan was to give Turkey control, but Britain had its own plan, which was to bring all the territories back under British rule, so they could choose what country they wanted to live in again. So there were two countries instead of just one.  The problem with this plan is that Turkey didn’t want it, especially when Churchill said “You can take Cyprus from us – you can have Greece and we will keep Cyprus”. They thought it would be a big mistake and Britain knew this, as did the Greeks.  Churchill then met with Makarios, the leader of the Greek Cypriots, to try and make peace between them, since this time they weren’t in conflict.  At the beginning of World War II, Churchill planned to invade Greece again, which would stop Germany getting supplies at the Dardanelles. This time, the allies invaded Greece, and managed to get rid of the Germans.  This meant Greece was free to join their ally and it was signed into law on May 6th 1941. It became a state known as ‘Free Greece’. There was little resistance from the population, since nobody wanted German occupation any longer. The only exception to this is Athens; there were many anti-German fighters from the city that were active for the rest of the war, even after Hitler declared Greece an occupied area.  Greece became a member of NATO in 1952, and Cyprus became a founding member of NATO in 1960. In 1963, Cyprus joined the European Economic Community (now the EU) and in 1973 entered the European Union.  In 1974, the Turkish invasion began and Cyprus remained divided until a referendum in 2002 changed the political status to a Republic.
